belt</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the album by Velvet       Chain, see <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_Belt_(album)" title="Asteroid Belt (album)">Asteroid Belt (album)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/450px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Solar_System" title="Inner Solar System">inner Solar System</a> and Jupiter: The donut-shaped asteroid belt is located between the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. <table style="width:100%;"> <tbody><tr> <td valign="top"> <span </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:301px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="897" data-file-width="1162" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png/299px-Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png/449px-Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png/598px-Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png 2x" width="299"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_asteroids_vs_main_belt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The relative masses of the top twelve asteroids known compared to the remaining mass of all the other asteroids in the belt.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4611" data-file-width="5888" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg/300px-Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg/450px-Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg/600px-Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres-Vesta-Eros_compared_to_Pluto-Charon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By far the largest object within the belt is <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>. The total mass of the asteroid belt is significantly less than <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>\'s, and approximately twice that of Pluto\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>asteroid belt</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disc" title="Circumstellar disc">circumstellar disc</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> located roughly between the orbits of the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. It is occupied by numerous irregularly shaped bodies called <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> or <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a>. The asteroid belt is also termed the <b>main asteroid belt</b> or <b>main belt</b> to distinguish it from other asteroid populations in the Solar System such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroids" title="Near-Earth asteroids">near-Earth asteroids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroids" title="Trojan asteroids">trojan asteroids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-1">[1]</a></sup> About half the mass of the belt is contained in the four largest asteroids: <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/10_Hygiea" title="10 Hygiea">Hygiea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-1">[1]</a></sup> The total mass of the asteroid belt is approximately 4% that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, or 22% that of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, and roughly twice that of Pluto\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> (whose diameter is 1200&#160;km).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, the asteroid belt\'s only <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>, is about 950&#160;km in diameter, whereas <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4&#160;Vesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2&#160;Pallas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/10_Hygiea" title="10 Hygiea">10&#160;Hygiea</a> have mean diameters of less than 600&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krasinskyetal2002_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinskyetal2002-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva2005-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halfmass_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-halfmass-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jplsbdb_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-jplsbdb-5">[5]</a></sup> The remaining bodies range down to the size of a dust particle. The asteroid material is so thinly distributed that numerous unmanned spacecraft have traversed it without incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Nonetheless, collisions between large asteroids do occur, and these can produce an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">asteroid family</a> whose members have similar orbital characteristics and compositions. Individual asteroids within the asteroid belt are categorized by their <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectra</a>, with most falling into three basic groups: <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbonaceous</a> (<a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">C-type</a>), <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> (<a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">S-type</a>), and metal-rich (<a href="/wiki/M-type_asteroid" title="M-type asteroid">M-type</a>).\n</p><p>The asteroid belt formed from the primordial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> as a group of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CosmosUp_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-CosmosUp-7">[7]</a></sup> Planetesimals are the smaller precursors of the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanets</a>. Between Mars and Jupiter, however, <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> perturbations from Jupiter imbued the protoplanets with too much orbital energy for them to <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accrete</a> into a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CosmosUp_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-CosmosUp-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Collisions became too violent, and instead of fusing together, the planetesimals and most of the protoplanets shattered. As a result, 99.9% of the asteroid belt\'s original mass was lost in the first 100&#160;million years of the Solar System\'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Some fragments eventually found their way into the inner <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, leading to meteorite impacts with the inner planets. Asteroid orbits continue to be appreciably <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbed</a> whenever their period of revolution about the Sun forms an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Jupiter. At these orbital distances, a <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gap</a> occurs as they are swept into other orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Classes of <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a> in other regions are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_objects" title="Near-Earth objects">near-Earth objects</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects, the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> objects, the <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">sednoids</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> objects.\n</p><p>On 22 January 2014, <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA scientists</a> reported the detection, for the first definitive time, of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> on Ceres, the largest object in the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K&#252;ppersO&#8217;Rourke2014_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-K&#252;ppersO&#8217;Rourke2014-11">[11]</a></sup> The detection was made by using the <a href="/wiki/Far-infrared_astronomy" title="Far-infrared astronomy">far-infrared abilities</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140122_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140122-12">[12]</a></sup> The finding was unexpected because <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, not asteroids, are typically considered to "sprout jets and plumes". According to one of the scientists, "The lines are becoming more and more blurred between comets and asteroids."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140122_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140122-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History_of_observation"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History of observation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Origin"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Origin</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Formation"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Formation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Evolution"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Evolution</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Characteristics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Composition"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Composition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Main-belt_comets"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Main-belt comets</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Orbits"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Orbits</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-9"><a href="#Kirkwood_gaps"><span class="tocnumber">3.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Kirkwood gaps</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Collisions"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Collisions</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Meteorites"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Meteorites</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Families_and_groups"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Families and groups</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Periphery"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Periphery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#New_families"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">New families</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Exploration"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Exploration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_observation">History of observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2060" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/150px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/225px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/300px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> was the person who first noticed in 1596 that there was something strange about the orbits of Mars and Jupiter.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">Definition of planet</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_minor_planets" title="List of minor planets">List of minor planets</a></div>\n<p>In 1596, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> predicted &#8220;Between Mars and Jupiter, I place a planet&#8221; in his <i><a href="/wiki/Mysterium_Cosmographicum" title="Mysterium Cosmographicum">Mysterium Cosmographicum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> While analyzing <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>\'s data, Kepler thought that there was too large a gap between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an anonymous footnote to his 1766 translation of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bonnet" title="Charles Bonnet">Charles Bonnet</a>\'s <i>Contemplation de la Nature</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asteroids_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-asteroids-15">[15]</a></sup> the astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Daniel_Titius" title="Johann Daniel Titius">Johann Daniel Titius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoskin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoskin-17">[17]</a></sup> noted an apparent pattern in the layout of the planets. If one began a numerical sequence at 0, then included 3, 6, 12, 24, 48, etc., doubling each time, and added four to each number and divided by 10, this produced a remarkably close approximation to the radii of the orbits of the known planets as measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_units" title="Astronomical units">astronomical units</a> <i>provided</i> one allowed for a "missing planet" (equivalent to 24 in the sequence) between the orbits of Mars (12) and Jupiter (48). In his footnote, Titius declared "But should the Lord Architect have left that space empty? Not at all."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawn_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> in 1781, the planet\'s orbit matched the law almost perfectly, leading astronomers to conclude that there had to be a planet between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="885" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg/150px-Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg/225px-Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg/300px-Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giuseppe_Piazzi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi" title="Giuseppe Piazzi">Giuseppe Piazzi</a>, discoverer of <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, the largest object in the asteroid belt. For several decades after its discovery, Ceres was known as a planet, after which it was reclassified as an asteroid. In 2006, it was designated as a dwarf planet.</div></div></div>\n<p>On January 1, 1801, Giuseppe Piazzi, chair of astronomy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Palermo" title="University of Palermo">University of Palermo</a>, Sicily, found a tiny moving object in an orbit with exactly the radius predicted by this pattern. He dubbed it "Ceres", after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceres_(Roman_mythology)" title="Ceres (Roman mythology)">Roman goddess</a> of the harvest and patron of Sicily. Piazzi initially believed it to be a comet, but its lack of a <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a> suggested it was a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-police_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-police-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thus, the aforementioned pattern, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law" title="Titius&#8211;Bode law">Titius&#8211;Bode law</a>, predicted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a> of all eight planets of the time (Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Ceres, Jupiter, Saturn and Uranus).\n</p><p>Fifteen months later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heinrich_Olbers" title="Heinrich Olbers">Heinrich Olbers</a> discovered a second object in the same region, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>. Unlike the other known planets, Ceres and Pallas remained points of light even under the highest telescope magnifications instead of resolving into discs. Apart from their rapid movement, they appeared indistinguishable from <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>.\n</p><p>Accordingly, in 1802, William Herschel suggested they be placed into a separate category, named "asteroids", after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> <i>asteroeides</i>, meaning "star-like".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aster-root_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-aster-root-20">[20]</a></sup>  Upon completing a series of observations of Ceres and Pallas, he concluded,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Neither the appellation of planets nor that of comets, can with any propriety of language be given to these two stars ... They resemble small stars so much as hardly to be distinguished from them. From this, their asteroidal appearance, if I take my name, and call them Asteroids; reserving for myself, however, the liberty of changing that name, if another, more expressive of their nature, should occur.\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>By 1807, further investigation revealed two new objects in the region: <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">Juno</a> and <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serendipity_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-serendipity-22">[22]</a></sup> The burning of <a href="/wiki/Lilienthal,_Lower_Saxony" title="Lilienthal, Lower Saxony">Lilienthal</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a>, where the main body of work had been done,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> brought this first period of discovery to a close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serendipity_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-serendipity-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite Herschel\'s coinage, for several decades it remained common practice to refer to these objects as planets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asteroids_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-asteroids-15">[15]</a></sup> and to prefix their names with numbers representing their date of discovery: 1&#160;Ceres, 2&#160;Pallas, 3&#160;Juno, 4&#160;Vesta. However, in 1845 astronomers detected a fifth object (<a href="/wiki/5_Astraea" title="5 Astraea">5&#160;Astraea</a>) and, shortly thereafter, new objects were found at an accelerating rate. Counting them among the planets became increasingly cumbersome. Eventually, they were dropped from the planet list (as first suggested by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> in the early 1850s) and Herschel\'s choice of nomenclature, "asteroids", gradually came into common use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asteroids_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-asteroids-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> in 1846 led to the discrediting of the Titius&#8211;Bode law in the eyes of scientists because its orbit was nowhere near the predicted position. To date, there is no scientific explanation for the law, and astronomers\' consensus regards it as a coincidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The expression "asteroid belt" came into use in the very early 1850s, although it is hard to pinpoint who coined the term. The first English use seems to be in the 1850 translation (by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._C._Ott%C3%A9" title="E. C. Ott&#233;">E. C. Ott&#233;</a>) of Alexander von Humboldt\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmos_(Humboldt)" title="Cosmos (Humboldt)">Cosmos</a></i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> "[...] and the regular appearance, about the 13th of November and the 11th of August, of shooting stars, which probably form part of a belt of asteroids intersecting the Earth\'s orbit and moving with planetary velocity". Another early appearance occurred in <a href="/wiki/Robert_James_Mann" title="Robert James Mann">Robert James Mann</a>\'s <i>A Guide to the Knowledge of the Heavens</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> "The orbits of the asteroids are placed in a wide belt of space, extending between the extremes of [...]". The American astronomer <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Peirce" title="Benjamin Peirce">Benjamin Peirce</a> seems to have adopted that terminology and to have been one of its promoters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ENPJ1857_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ENPJ1857-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One hundred asteroids had been located by mid-1868, and in 1891 the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Astrophotography" title="Astrophotography">astrophotography</a> by <a href="/wiki/Max_Wolf" title="Max Wolf">Max Wolf</a> accelerated the rate of discovery still further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> A total of 1,000 asteroids had been found by 1921,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> 10,000 by 1981,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and 100,000 by 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Modern asteroid survey systems now use automated means to locate new minor planets in ever-increasing quantities.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_belt_i_vs_a.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="834" data-file-width="1067" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Main_belt_i_vs_a.png/300px-Main_belt_i_vs_a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Main_belt_i_vs_a.png/450px-Main_belt_i_vs_a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Main_belt_i_vs_a.png/600px-Main_belt_i_vs_a.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_belt_i_vs_a.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The asteroid belt showing the orbital inclinations versus distances from the Sun, with asteroids in the core region of the asteroid belt in red and other asteroids in blue</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1802, shortly after discovering Pallas, Olbers suggested to Herschel that Ceres and Pallas were <a href="/wiki/Phaeton_(hypothetical_planet)" title="Phaeton (hypothetical planet)">fragments of a much larger planet</a> that once occupied the Mars&#8211;Jupiter region, this planet having suffered an internal explosion or a cometary impact many million years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The large amount of energy required to destroy a planet, combined with the belt\'s low combined mass, which is only about 4% of the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krasinskyetal2002_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinskyetal2002-2">[2]</a></sup> do not support the hypothesis. Further, the significant chemical differences between the asteroids become difficult to explain if they come from the same planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> As of 2018, a study was released from researchers at the University of Florida that found the asteroid belt was created from the remnants of several ancient planets instead of a singular planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A hypothesis to the asteroid belt creation is that in general, in the Solar System, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_formation" title="Planetary formation">planetary formation</a> is thought to have occurred via a process comparable to the long-standing nebular hypothesis: a cloud of interstellar dust and gas collapsed under the influence of gravity to form a rotating disc of material that then further condensed to form the Sun and planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> During the first few million years of the Solar System\'s history, an <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> process of sticky collisions caused the clumping of small particles, which gradually increased in size. Once the clumps reached sufficient mass, they could draw in other bodies through gravitational attraction and become <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>. This gravitational accretion led to the formation of the planets.\n</p><p>Planetesimals within the region which would become the asteroid belt were too strongly <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbed</a> by Jupiter\'s gravity to form a planet. Instead, they continued to orbit the Sun as before, occasionally colliding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus153_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus153-36">[36]</a></sup> In regions where the average velocity of the collisions was too high, the shattering of planetesimals tended to dominate over accretion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> preventing the formation of planet-sized bodies. <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">Orbital resonances</a> occurred where the orbital period of an object in the belt formed an integer fraction of the orbital period of Jupiter, perturbing the object into a different orbit; the region lying between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter contains many such orbital resonances. As <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Jupiter migrated inward</a> following its formation, these resonances would have swept across the asteroid belt, dynamically exciting the region\'s population and increasing their velocities relative to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the early history of the Solar System, the asteroids melted to some degree, allowing elements within them to be partially or completely differentiated by mass. Some of the progenitor bodies may even have undergone periods of explosive <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> and formed <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> oceans. However, because of the relatively small size of the bodies, the period of melting was necessarily brief (compared to the much larger planets), and had generally ended about 4.5&#160;billion years ago, in the first tens of millions of years of formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In August 2007, a study of <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">zircon</a> crystals in an Antarctic meteorite believed to have originated from Vesta suggested that it, and by extension the rest of the asteroid belt, had formed rather quickly, within 10&#160;million years of the Solar System\'s origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The asteroids are not samples of the primordial Solar System. They have undergone considerable evolution since their formation, including internal heating (in the first few tens of millions of years), surface melting from impacts, <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">space weathering</a> from radiation, and bombardment by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometeorites" title="Micrometeorites">micrometeorites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Although some scientists refer to the asteroids as residual planetesimals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> other scientists consider them distinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The current asteroid belt is believed to contain only a small fraction of the mass of the primordial belt. Computer simulations suggest that the original asteroid belt may have contained the mass equivalent to the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>  Primarily because of gravitational perturbations, most of the material was ejected from the belt within about 1&#160;million years of formation, leaving behind less than 0.1% of the original mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus153_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-icarus153-36">[36]</a></sup> Since their formation, the size distribution of the asteroid belt has remained relatively stable: there has been no significant increase or decrease in the typical dimensions of the main-belt asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 4:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Jupiter, at a radius 2.06&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>, can be considered the inner boundary of the asteroid belt. Perturbations by Jupiter send bodies straying there into unstable orbits. Most bodies formed within the radius of this gap were swept up by <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> (which has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> at 1.67&#160;AU) or ejected by its gravitational perturbations in the early history of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungaria_asteroids" title="Hungaria asteroids">Hungaria asteroids</a> lie closer to the Sun than the 4:1 resonance, but are protected from disruption by their high inclination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the asteroid belt was first formed, the temperatures at a distance of 2.7&#160;AU from the Sun formed a "<a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">snow line</a>"  below the freezing point of water. Planetesimals formed beyond this radius were able to accumulate ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\nIn 2006 it was announced that a population of <a href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">comets</a> had been discovered within the asteroid belt beyond the snow line, which may have provided a source of water for Earth\'s oceans. According to some models, there was insufficient <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> of water during the Earth\'s formative period to form the oceans, requiring an external source such as a cometary bombardment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:951_Gaspra.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="945" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/200px-951_Gaspra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/300px-951_Gaspra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/400px-951_Gaspra.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951&#160;Gaspra</a>, the first asteroid imaged by a spacecraft, as viewed during <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a>\'</i>s 1991 flyby; colors are exaggerated</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AllendeMeteorite.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/AllendeMeteorite.jpg/200px-AllendeMeteorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/AllendeMeteorite.jpg/300px-AllendeMeteorite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/AllendeMeteorite.jpg/400px-AllendeMeteorite.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Fragment of the <a href="/wiki/Allende_meteorite" title="Allende meteorite">Allende meteorite</a>, a carbonaceous chondrite that fell to Earth in Mexico in 1969</div></div></div></div>\n<p>Contrary to popular imagery, the asteroid belt is mostly empty. The asteroids are spread over such a large volume that it would be improbable to reach an asteroid without aiming carefully. Nonetheless, hundreds of thousands of asteroids are currently known, and the total number ranges in the millions or more, depending on the lower size cutoff. Over 200 asteroids are known to be larger than 100&#160;km,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and a survey in the infrared wavelengths has shown that the asteroid belt has between 700,000 and 1.7&#160;million asteroids with a diameter of 1&#160;km or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitudes</a> of most of the known asteroids are between 11 and 19, with the median at about 16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpc-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The total mass of the asteroid belt is estimated to be  between <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7021280000000000000&#9824;</span>2.8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>21</sup></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7021320000000000000&#9824;</span>3.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>21</sup></span> kilograms, which is just 4% of the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva2005_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva2005-3">[3]</a></sup> The four largest objects, <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4&#160;Vesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2&#160;Pallas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/10_Hygiea" title="10 Hygiea">10&#160;Hygiea</a>, account for half of the belt\'s total mass, with almost one-third accounted for by Ceres alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halfmass_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-halfmass-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jplsbdb_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-jplsbdb-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The current belt consists primarily of three categories of asteroids: C-type or carbonaceous asteroids, S-type or silicate asteroids, and M-type or metallic asteroids.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">Carbonaceous asteroids</a>, as their name suggests, are carbon-rich. They dominate the asteroid belt\'s outer regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApJ133_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApJ133-54">[54]</a></sup> Together they comprise over 75% of the visible asteroids. They are redder in hue than the other asteroids and have a very low <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. Their surface composition is similar to <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. Chemically, their spectra match the primordial composition of the early Solar System, with only the lighter elements and <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a> removed.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">S-type</a> (<a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a>-rich) asteroids are more common toward the inner region of the belt, within 2.5&#160;AU of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApJ133_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-ApJ133-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The spectra of their surfaces reveal the presence of silicates and some metal, but no significant carbonaceous compounds. This indicates that their materials have been significantly modified from their primordial composition, probably through melting and reformation. They have a relatively high albedo and form about 17% of the total asteroid population.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/M-type_asteroid" title="M-type asteroid">M-type</a> (metal-rich) asteroids form about 10% of the total population; their spectra resemble that of iron-nickel. Some are believed to have formed from the metallic cores of differentiated progenitor bodies that were disrupted through collision. However, there are also some silicate compounds that can produce a similar appearance. For example, the large M-type asteroid <a href="/wiki/22_Kalliope" title="22 Kalliope">22&#160;Kalliope</a> does not appear to be primarily composed of metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Within the asteroid belt, the number distribution of M-type asteroids peaks at a semi-major axis of about 2.7&#160;AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang2003_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang2003-57">[57]</a></sup> It is not yet clear whether all M-types are compositionally similar, or whether it is a label for several varieties which do not fit neatly into the main C and S classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2052" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg/220px-Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg/330px-Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg/440px-Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_views_extraordinary_multi-tailed_asteroid_P2013_P5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a> views extraordinary multi-tailed asteroid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P/2013_P5" title="P/2013 P5">P/2013&#160;P5</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>One mystery of the asteroid belt is the relative rarity of <a href="/wiki/V-type_asteroid" title="V-type asteroid">V-type</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basaltic" title="Basaltic">basaltic</a> asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffard_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duffard-60">[60]</a></sup>  Theories of asteroid formation predict that objects the size of Vesta or larger should form crusts and mantles, which would be composed mainly of basaltic rock, resulting in more than half of all asteroids being composed either of basalt or <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>. Observations, however, suggest that 99 percent of the predicted basaltic material is missing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-61">[61]</a></sup>  Until 2001, most basaltic bodies discovered in the asteroid belt were believed to originate from the asteroid Vesta (hence their name V-type). However, the discovery of the asteroid <a href="/wiki/1459_Magnya" title="1459 Magnya">1459&#160;Magnya</a> revealed a slightly different chemical composition from the other basaltic asteroids discovered until then, suggesting a different origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-61">[61]</a></sup> This hypothesis was reinforced by the further discovery in 2007 of two asteroids in the outer belt, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7472_Kumakiri" title="7472 Kumakiri">7472&#160;Kumakiri</a> and <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/(10537)_1991_RY16" title="(10537) 1991 RY16">(10537) 1991 RY<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">16</span></span></a></span>, with a differing basaltic composition that could not have originated from Vesta. These latter two are the only V-type asteroids discovered in the outer belt to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffard_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Duffard-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The temperature of the asteroid belt varies with the distance from the Sun. For dust particles within the belt, typical temperatures range from 200&#160;K (&#8722;73&#160;&#176;C) at 2.2&#160;AU down to 165&#160;K (&#8722;108&#160;&#176;C) at 3.2&#160;AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> However, due to rotation, the surface temperature of an asteroid can vary considerably as the sides are alternately exposed to solar radiation and then to the stellar background.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main-belt_comets">Main-belt comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Main-belt comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">Main-belt comet</a></div>\n<p>Several otherwise unremarkable bodies in the outer belt show <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">cometary</a> activity. Because their orbits cannot be explained through the capture of classical comets, it is thought that many of the outer asteroids may be icy, with the ice occasionally exposed to sublimation through small impacts. Main-belt comets may have been a major source of the Earth\'s oceans because the deuterium-hydrogen ratio is too low for classical comets to have been the principal source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbits">Orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_belt_e_vs_a.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="834" data-file-width="1086" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Main_belt_e_vs_a.png/300px-Main_belt_e_vs_a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Main_belt_e_vs_a.png/450px-Main_belt_e_vs_a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Main_belt_e_vs_a.png/600px-Main_belt_e_vs_a.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_belt_e_vs_a.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The asteroid belt (showing eccentricities), with the asteroid belt in red and blue ("core" region in red)</div></div></div>\n<p>Most asteroids within the asteroid belt have orbital eccentricities of less than 0.4, and an inclination of less than 30&#176;. The orbital distribution of the asteroids reaches a maximum at an eccentricity of around 0.07 and an inclination below 4&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-mpc-53">[53]</a></sup> Thus although a typical asteroid has a relatively circular orbit and lies near the plane of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>, some asteroid orbits can be highly eccentric or travel well outside the ecliptic plane.\n</p><p>Sometimes, the term <i>main belt</i> is used to refer only to the more compact "core" region where the greatest concentration of bodies is found. This lies between the strong 4:1 and 2:1 <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gaps</a> at 2.06 and 3.27&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>, and at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(orbit)" title="Eccentricity (orbit)">orbital eccentricities</a> less than roughly 0.33, along with orbital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclinations</a> below about 20&#176;. As of  2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, this "core" region contained 93% of all discovered and numbered minor planets within the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basedon1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-basedon1-64">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/JPL_Small-Body_Database" title="JPL Small-Body Database">JPL Small-Body Database</a> lists over 670,000 known main belt asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL-MBA_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL-MBA-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kirkwood_gaps">Kirkwood gaps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kirkwood gaps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gap</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kirkwood_Gaps.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="792" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Kirkwood_Gaps.svg/300px-Kirkwood_Gaps.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Kirkwood_Gaps.svg/450px-Kirkwood_Gaps.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Kirkwood_Gaps.svg/600px-Kirkwood_Gaps.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kirkwood_Gaps.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Number of asteroids in the asteroid belt as a function of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a>. The dashed lines indicate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gaps" title="Kirkwood gaps">Kirkwood gaps</a>, where orbital resonances with <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> destabilize orbits. The color gives a possible division into three zones:<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #333; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#005aff; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Zone I: inner main-belt (<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Semi-major_and_semi-minor_axes" title="Semi-major and semi-minor axes"><i>a</i></a> &lt; 2.5 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span>)</span><br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #222; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffa500; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Zone II: middle main-belt (<span class="nowrap">2.5 AU &lt; <i>a</i> &lt; 2.82 AU</span>)</span><br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #333; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#55d400; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Zone III: outer main-belt (<span class="nowrap"><i>a</i> &gt; 2.82 AU</span>)</span></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Semi-major_and_semi-minor_axes" title="Semi-major and semi-minor axes">semi-major axis</a> of an asteroid is used to describe the dimensions of its orbit around the Sun, and its value determines the minor planet\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a>. In 1866, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kirkwood" title="Daniel Kirkwood">Daniel Kirkwood</a> announced the discovery of gaps in the distances of these bodies\' orbits from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. They were located in positions where their period of revolution about the Sun was an integer fraction of Jupiter\'s orbital period. Kirkwood proposed that the gravitational perturbations of the planet led to the removal of asteroids from these orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the mean orbital period of an asteroid is an integer fraction of the orbital period of Jupiter, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean-motion_resonance" title="Mean-motion resonance">mean-motion resonance</a> with the gas giant is created that is sufficient to perturb an asteroid to new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_element" title="Orbital element">orbital elements</a>. Asteroids that become located in the gap orbits (either primordially because of the migration of Jupiter\'s orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> or due to prior perturbations or collisions) are gradually nudged into different, random orbits with a larger or smaller semi-major axis.\n</p><p>The gaps are not seen in a simple snapshot of the locations of the asteroids at any one time because asteroid orbits are elliptical, and many asteroids still cross through the radii corresponding to the gaps. The actual spatial density of asteroids in these gaps does not differ significantly from the neighboring regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnras244_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-mnras244-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main gaps occur at the 3:1, 5:2, 7:3, and 2:1 mean-motion resonances with Jupiter. An asteroid in the 3:1 Kirkwood gap would orbit the Sun three times for each Jovian orbit, for instance. Weaker resonances occur at other semi-major axis values, with fewer asteroids found than nearby. (For example, an 8:3 resonance for asteroids with a semi-major axis of 2.71&#160;AU.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau160_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau160-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main or core population of the asteroid belt is sometimes divided into three zones, based on the most prominent Kirkwood gaps:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Zone I lies between the 4:1 resonance (2.06&#160;AU) and 3:1 resonance (2.5&#160;AU) Kirkwood gaps.</li>\n<li>Zone II continues from the end of Zone I out to the 5:2 resonance gap (2.82&#160;AU).</li>\n<li>Zone III extends from the outer edge of Zone II to the 2:1 resonance gap (3.28&#160;AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The asteroid belt may also be divided into the inner and outer belts, with the inner belt formed by asteroids orbiting nearer to Mars than the 3:1 Kirkwood gap (2.5&#160;AU), and the outer belt formed by those asteroids closer to Jupiter\'s orbit. (Some authors subdivide the inner and outer belts at the 2:1 resonance gap (3.3&#160;AU), whereas others suggest inner, middle, and outer belts; also see diagram).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collisions">Collisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Collisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zodiacal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="324" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Zodiacal.jpg/220px-Zodiacal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Zodiacal.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zodiacal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>, a minor part of which is created by dust from collisions in the asteroid belt</div></div></div>\n<p>The high population of the asteroid belt makes for a very active environment, where collisions between asteroids occur frequently (on astronomical time scales). Collisions between main-belt bodies with a mean radius of 10&#160;km are expected to occur about once every 10&#160;million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backman_report_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-backman_report-71">[71]</a></sup> A collision may fragment an asteroid into numerous smaller pieces (leading to the formation of a new <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">asteroid family</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Conversely, collisions that occur at low relative speeds may also join two asteroids. After more than 4&#160;billion years of such processes, the members of the asteroid belt now bear little resemblance to the original population.\n</p><p>Along with the asteroid bodies, the asteroid belt also contains bands of dust with particle radii of up to a few hundred <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres</a>. This fine material is produced, at least in part, from collisions between asteroids, and by the impact of micrometeorites upon the asteroids. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Poynting%E2%80%93Robertson_effect" title="Poynting&#8211;Robertson effect">Poynting&#8211;Robertson effect</a>, the pressure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> causes this dust to slowly spiral inward toward the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj392_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj392-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combination of this fine asteroid dust, as well as ejected cometary material, produces the <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>. This faint auroral glow can be viewed at night extending from the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> along the plane of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. Asteroid particles that produce the visible zodiacal light average about 40&#160;&#956;m in radius. The typical lifetimes of main-belt zodiacal cloud particles are about 700,000&#160;years. Thus, to maintain the bands of dust, new particles must be steadily produced within the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj392_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-apj392-73">[73]</a></sup> It was once thought that collisions of asteroids form a major component of the <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>.  However, computer simulations by Nesvorn&#253; and colleagues attributed 85 percent of the zodiacal-light dust to fragmentations of Jupiter-family comets, rather than to comets and collisions between asteroids in the asteroid belt. At most 10 percent of the dust is attributed to the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zodiacallightJFC_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-zodiacallightJFC-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorites">Meteorites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Meteorites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some of the debris from collisions can form <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a> that enter the Earth\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Of the 50,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">meteorites</a> found on Earth to date, 99.8 percent are believed to have originated in the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Families_and_groups">Families and groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Families and groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">Asteroid family</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="842" data-file-width="1037" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png/300px-Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png/450px-Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png/600px-Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_proper_elements_i_vs_e.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This plot of orbital inclination (<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) versus eccentricity (<i>e<sub>p</sub></i>) for the numbered main-belt asteroids clearly shows clumpings representing asteroid families.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1918, the Japanese astronomer <a href="/wiki/Kiyotsugu_Hirayama" title="Kiyotsugu Hirayama">Kiyotsugu Hirayama</a> noticed that the orbits of some of the asteroids had similar parameters, forming families or groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Approximately one-third of the asteroids in the asteroid belt are members of an asteroid family. These share similar orbital elements, such as semi-major axis, eccentricity, and orbital inclination as well as similar spectral features, all of which indicate a common origin in the breakup of a larger body. Graphical displays of these elements, for members of the asteroid belt, show concentrations indicating the presence of an asteroid family. There are about 20 to 30 associations that are almost certainly asteroid families. Additional groupings have been found that are less certain. Asteroid families can be confirmed when the members display common spectral features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Smaller associations of asteroids are called groups or clusters.\n</p><p>Some of the most prominent families in the asteroid belt (in order of increasing semi-major axes) are the <a href="/wiki/Flora_family" title="Flora family">Flora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eunomia_family" title="Eunomia family">Eunoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">Koronis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eos_family" title="Eos family">Eos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Themis_family" title="Themis family">Themis</a> families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang2003_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-lang2003-57">[57]</a></sup> The Flora family, one of the largest with more than 800 known members, may have formed from a collision less than 1&#160;billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\nThe largest asteroid to be a true member of a family (as opposed to an interloper in the case of Ceres with the <a href="/wiki/Gefion_family" title="Gefion family">Gefion family</a>) is 4&#160;Vesta. The <a href="/wiki/Vesta_family" title="Vesta family">Vesta family</a> is believed to have formed as the result of a crater-forming impact on Vesta. Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">HED meteorites</a> may also have originated from Vesta as a result of this collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three prominent bands of dust have been found within the asteroid belt. These have similar orbital inclinations as the Eos, Koronis, and Themis asteroid families, and so are possibly associated with those groupings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main belt evolution after the Late Heavy Bombardment was very likely affected by the passages of large Centaurs and trans-Neptunian objects (TNOs).\nCentaurs and TNOs that reach the inner Solar System can modify the orbits of main belt asteroids, though only if their mass is of the order of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7021198855000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>&#8722;9</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>&#9737;</sub></a></span> for single encounters or, one order less in case of multiple close encounters. \nHowever Centaurs and TNOs are unlikely to have significantly dispersed young asteroid families in the main belt, but they can have perturbed some old asteroid families. Current main belt asteroids that originated as Centaurs or trans-Neptunian objects may lie in the outer belt with short lifetime of less than 4&#160;million years, most likely between 2.8 and 3.2&#160;AU at larger eccentricities than typical of main belt asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periphery">Periphery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Periphery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Skirting the inner edge of the belt (ranging between 1.78 and 2.0&#160;AU, with a mean semi-major axis of 1.9&#160;AU) is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungaria_family" title="Hungaria family">Hungaria family</a> of minor planets. They are named after the main member, <a href="/wiki/434_Hungaria" title="434 Hungaria">434&#160;Hungaria</a>; the group contains at least 52 named asteroids. The Hungaria group is separated from the main body by the 4:1 Kirkwood gap and their orbits have a high inclination. Some members belong to the Mars-crossing category of asteroids, and gravitational perturbations by Mars are likely a factor in reducing the total population of this group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another high-inclination group in the inner part of the asteroid belt is the <a href="/wiki/Phocaea_family" title="Phocaea family">Phocaea family</a>. These are composed primarily of S-type asteroids, whereas the neighboring Hungaria family includes some <a href="/wiki/E-type_asteroid" title="E-type asteroid">E-types</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The Phocaea family orbit between 2.25 and 2.5&#160;AU from the Sun.\n</p><p>Skirting the outer edge of the asteroid belt is the <a href="/wiki/Cybele_asteroid" title="Cybele asteroid">Cybele group</a>, orbiting between 3.3 and 3.5&#160;AU. These have a 7:4 orbital resonance with Jupiter. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">Hilda family</a> orbit between 3.5 and 4.2&#160;AU, and have relatively circular orbits and a stable 3:2 orbital resonance with Jupiter. There are few asteroids beyond 4.2&#160;AU, until Jupiter\'s orbit. Here the two families of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">Trojan asteroids</a> can be found, which, at least for objects larger than 1&#160;km, are approximately as numerous as the asteroids of the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_families">New families</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New families">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some asteroid families have formed recently, in astronomical terms. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karin_Cluster" title="Karin Cluster">Karin Cluster</a> apparently formed about 5.7&#160;million years ago from a collision with a progenitor asteroid 33&#160;km in radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/490_Veritas" title="490 Veritas">Veritas family</a> formed about 8.3&#160;million years ago; evidence includes interplanetary dust recovered from ocean sediment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More recently, the <a href="/wiki/1270_Datura" title="1270 Datura">Datura cluster</a> appears to have formed about 530,000 years ago from a collision with a main-belt asteroid. The age estimate is based on the probability of the members having their current orbits, rather than from any physical evidence. However, this cluster may have been a source for some zodiacal dust material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Other recent cluster formations, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4652_Iannini" title="4652 Iannini">Iannini cluster</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1&#8211;5</span>&#160;million years ago), may have provided additional sources of this asteroid dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1147" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/220px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/330px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/440px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s concept of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> spacecraft with <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first spacecraft to traverse the asteroid belt was <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer&#160;10</a></i>, which entered the region on 16 July 1972. At the time there was some concern that the debris in the belt would pose a hazard to the spacecraft, but it has since been safely traversed by 12 spacecraft without incident. <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer&#160;11</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyagers&#160;1</i> and <i>2</i></a> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulysses_probe" title="Ulysses probe">Ulysses</a></i> passed through the belt without imaging any asteroids. <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> imaged <a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951&#160;Gaspra</a> in 1991 and <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243&#160;Ida</a> in 1993, <a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR</a> imaged <a href="/wiki/253_Mathilde" title="253 Mathilde">253&#160;Mathilde</a> in 1997 and landed on 433 Eros in February 2001, <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> imaged <a href="/wiki/2685_Masursky" title="2685 Masursky">2685&#160;Masursky</a> in 2000, <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></i> imaged <a href="/wiki/5535_Annefrank" title="5535 Annefrank">5535&#160;Annefrank</a> in 2002, <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> imaged <a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524&#160;APL</a> in 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">Rosetta</a></i> imaged <a href="/wiki/2867_%C5%A0teins" title="2867 &#352;teins">2867&#160;&#352;teins</a> in September 2008 and <a href="/wiki/21_Lutetia" title="21 Lutetia">21&#160;Lutetia</a> in July 2010, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> orbited <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> between July 2011 and September 2012 and has orbited <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> since March 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> On its way to Jupiter, <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> traversed the asteroid belt without collecting science data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Due to the low density of materials within the belt, the odds of a probe running into an asteroid are now estimated at less than 1 in 1&#160;billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most belt asteroids imaged to date have come from brief <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> opportunities by probes headed for other targets. Only the <i>Dawn</i>, NEAR and <i><a href="/wiki/Hayabusa" title="Hayabusa">Hayabusa</a></i> missions have studied asteroids for a protracted period in orbit and at the surface.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><ul style="font-weight: bold">\n<li><a href="/wiki/Book:Solar_System" title="Book:Solar System">Book: Solar System</a></li>\n</ul></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">Asteroid mining</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Asteroids_in_fiction" title="Asteroids in fiction">Asteroids in fiction</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_asteroids" title="Colonization of the asteroids">Colonization of the asteroids</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Debris_disk" title="Debris disk">Debris disk</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Disrupted_planet" title="Disrupted planet">Disrupted planet</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_asteroids_in_astrology" title="List of asteroids in astrology">List of asteroids in astrology</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_exceptional_asteroids" title="List of exceptional asteroids">List of exceptional asteroids</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> (The other ring of material, at about 30-60 AU)</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid_belt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
